B00064WSOQ|5.0|This is supposedly King Crimson's last studio effort, since Robert Fripp announced his retirement from music last year*.  Whether or not he is actually gone for good this time, The Power to Believe feels like a great note to go out on.  It's easily the band's strongest effort since 1981's Discipline, and comfortably stands next to their classic albums while still staying modern and perhaps even futuristic.<br /><br />The overall sound of this album is fairly heavy for the most part; compare to 1974's Red or their previous two efforts.  Tracks like \\"Level Five\\" and \\"Elektrik\\" will be instant favorites of anyone who loves the crunchy guitar tone Fripp uses on those records.  The album's not devoid of subtlety, though; \\"The Power To Believe II\\" reminds more of the softer moments on Larks' Tongues in Aspic, while \\"Eyes Wide Open\\" harkens slightly toward Three of a Perfect Pair (albeit a much better song than anything on that album).  Indeed, this album seems to utilize some aspect or another of just about every King Crimson album before it; it's got the epic instrumentals, the poppier songs, and even the quirky little deep cuts that are somewhere in between.<br /><br />The musicians on this album are as extraordinary as on any past King Crimson release.  Robert Fripp is a guitar god, and his work on this album strongly solidifies that status.  Adrian Belew is often seen as the physical line dividing diehard fans of KC's early work and fans of their work from the 80's onward due to his singing style and occasionally silly lyrics, but it's undeniable that he is one of the few who can stand next to Fripp with guitar in hand and not look idiotic.  Trey Gunn is back with his unusual Warr guitar, which is basically a bass with about three times as many strings, making for some interesting textures.  However, it's Pat Mastelotto who gets the most attention from me, being a drummer myself.  His play style is similar to previous KC drummer Bill Bruford's in that he can take an odd time signature and make it feel very natural.  What sets him apart is his inventive use of electronic drums alongside acoustic ones; sometimes it's difficult to tell which is which.<br /><br />For those who are interested and for the sake of thoroughness, what follows is a track-by-track review.  If you are at all worried about having the album spoiled for you, I would stop reading here.<br /><br />*EDIT: Robert Fripp announced the resurrection of King Crimson the day after I posted this.  It seems I spoke a bit too soon.  :P<br /><br />---------------SPOILER ALERT BAR---------------<br /><br />1.The Power to Believe I: A Cappella - No music, just a short poem sung by Belew that acts as an intro to the album.  This poem is a recurring theme of the album, and appears three more times before the album is over.<br /><br />2.Level Five - This is where the real music starts.  You may have turned the volume up on your stereo a bit to hear the quiet first track better, and if you did you're in for a big surprise, as this song immediately grabs you by the throat and doesn't let go for 7 minutes.  Heavy, angular rhythms are made all the more intriguing by a mixture of electronic and acoustic drums.<br /><br />3.Eyes Wide Open - Vocals and music finally come together.  This is one of King Crimson's \\"poppier\\" songs, but it's not devoid of KC's trademark odd time signatures or progressions.  This is the one you're most likely to find yourself humming subconsciously.<br /><br />4.Elektrik - Another lengthy instrumental and my favorite track on the album.  There is a nice balance of loud and quiet moments on this song that give it a great sense of dynamics.  This is also perhaps the most rhythmically interesting song on the album; the band plows through quite a few time signatures, and Mastelotto gets to show off his hybrid kit.<br /><br />5.Facts of Life: Intro - The extended intro to the next song.  A good cool-down moment after Elektrik.  Sometimes I'm tempted to skip it toget on with the actual song, though.<br /><br />6.Facts of Life - The second \\"pop\\" song (I use this term VERY loosely) starts with Adrian Belew singing in a mock Tom Waits fashion over an 11/4 against 4/4 polyrhythm between drums and guitars.  This is the easiest track to headbang to despite the odd time signatures.  Awesome bridge.<br /><br />7.The Power to Believe II - The song starts off sounding like the sonic representation of walking through a desert while on acid, then morphs into a middle-eastern percussive jam.  The theme poem from the first track makes its second appearance, paving the way for an authentic Fripp guitar solo that floats on top of the wall of percussion, bass, and synth.<br /><br />8.Dangerous Curves - Another slowly building piece.  Starts as a whisper and builds to an explosive ending.  The hypnotic, rhythmic guitar riff is very cool.  Reminds me a lot of \\"The Talking Drum\\" from Larks' Tongues in Aspic.  However, oftentimes I feel that the payoff doesn't live up to the buildup.<br /><br />9.Happy With What You Have To Be Happy With - This song seems to poke fun at modern hard rock and nu metal.  It's a bit \\"love it or hate it\\".  I think it's a good song, but sometimes it feels like too much of a joke.  If there is any single moment that the album loses momentum, it's here.<br /><br />10.The Power to Believe III - The theme poem is back (well, sort of), this time amid a soft cloud of synth, which is occasionally interrupted by a loud electronic drum beat that may make you pee your pants if you have the volume turned up and aren't expecting it.  The song takes a turn for the heavier halfway through with another soaring guitar solo.  Really cool piece, but I wish it was longer, as it seems to end just as it's getting started.<br /><br />11.The Power to Believe IV: Coda - The fourth and final reprise.  The cloud of synth returns more confident than before, and carries the album to a natural conclusion as the central poem is repeated one lasttime.

B00ECU34ZM|3.0|When you have seen as many movies as I have you tend to become jaded. You wonder if an original blockbuster-type movie will ever be made with genuine effort and a non-ironic purpose, especially in the sci-fi genre. You fear the day will never come. It is nice to be proven wrong.<br /><br />Oblivion is not a fantastic movie by any means. It has its problems. There are some inconsistencies and goofs here and there, but you can tell from the first few minutes that genuine thought and effort was put in to this movie, and that more than makes up for its other issues. There is a style and an atmosphere to it. In a time when green-screen rooms are substituted for real sets so often that you begin to worry for the livelihoods of studio carpenters, here is a film which \\"feels\\" real. These are sets and locations that, though parts of them were C.G., there was a seamless blending with the real that is a treat for computer weary eyes.<br /><br />Tom Cruise plays Jack Harper, a technician responsible for the maintenance and security of huge reactors that supply power to off-world colonists. Earth has been nearly destroyed by an invading alien force and, though humanity won the war, the planet is a wasteland. This is explained by a helpful (if annoying) voice over/scroll at the beginning of the film. Jack's only company is his communications officer Victoria (Andrea Riseborough) with whom he occasionally gets frisky. Their memories are periodically erased to protect vital off-world information in case of capture by the remnants of the alien force which they call \\"Scavs\\". In spite of their memory wipes Jack has flashes of his life from before the war, which worries him because the war ended sixty years ago, well before he was born.<br /><br />His companion tells him to focus on his work and not to worry. After all, they are leaving for the new colony in two weeks and they don't want to ruin their chance to rejoin humanity. The work continues as usual until an encounter with the Scavs and the discovery of a crashed spaceship shake everything Jack thinks he knows.<br /><br />From here we would enter into spoiler territory, so I will dispense with the exposition. Suffice it to say that what follows, while not perfect, at least shows true effort at the writing, acting, directing, and effects levels. The actors really care about what they are doing and it shows. Tom Cruise, of course, can only play Tom Cruise. His range is limited, but here it is well used. The mystery of the plot, while a little predictable, at least doesn't insult the viewer's intelligence. In a limited fashion the movie brings up questions about what it means to be human and what our purpose really is in the world. Though these issues are not delved into too deeply, it was nice to see them actually dealt with and not just used as cheap set pieces. The set designers and the director take care to create a world we can dive into and almost touch and taste. The sterility of Jack's base really contrasts with the grittiness of the devastated world below.<br /><br />The only items I would change are relatively minor. One is the opening scroll/narration. Opening scrolls may have been cool in Star Wars a few decades ago, but now they are a little dated and annoying. They are especially grating when read aloud to us and doubly so when you divulge the exact same information with dialogue not too long after. The other change would be to distinguish between the female leads physically a little better. Because of the washed out color scheme of the movie it is hard to tell the two women apart at times and it gets distracting. Is that her? No, wait. It's the other one. The fact that the two women are usually in different styles of clothing and in different locations helps, but it takes a while to get there.<br /><br />This is a movie well worth the effort put into it. It is good sci-fi with good execution and no franchise to prop it up. The studio went out on a limb by not playing it safe andfinding something to reboot or a big name to tag a sequel onto. Here's hoping this movie gets a lot of attention and maybe we will see more of its kind in the future.

B006CEYM3Y|5.0|Trajan is a great board game with some very unique elements.  One the one hand, it looks and plays like any other European worker placement game.  On the other hand, the action selection mechanic is truly unique and adds a lot of interesting strategic options.  Rather than simply choosing an action based on availability/cost/etc., players move wooden tokens around a circular track with &#34;pits&#34; in it, a la mancala.  The action pit you end up in is the one you take.  Furthering this mechanic is the fact that the wooden tokens are colored and the color combination that you end up with can get you bonus actions and points if you have a matching Trajan tile placed above the action you choose.  It sounds quite complicated, and on some level, it is, but it isn't too hard to learn the game overall.<br />Due to its unique mechanics and deep strategy, I've had a great time playing Trajan so far, and will no doubt continue to enjoy it for years to come!

Closer to the Truth is by no means a bad album, make no mistake about it. In fact I'm actually really enjoying it and predict it won't leave my player for a while. But at the same time, I'd be lying if I said it wasn't exactly the kind of dance/pop album I expected it to be, with no additional frills or surprises. The thing is, Believe and Living Proof, brilliant and groundbreaking as they were in their day, followed very specific formulas which, fast forward to 2013, Cher has strayed very little from. Again, that isn't necessarily a criticism, but don't go into it expecting some grand reinvention or that she has spent all this time secretly plotting the next big trend. If you have kept up with Cher at all since about 1998, this should be familiar territory to you.<br /><br />So with that said, I'm still very pleased with what she has delivered here. The bulk of the album is comprised of catchy, upbeat tracks that are definitely club-oriented, yet also successfully fuses elements of different genres together to forge identities of their own. Some moments even hark back to the more adult contemporary, \\"rocker chick\\" Cher of the 80's, which I know many fans were eager to see resurface again. Ironically, it's the first single, Woman's World, that is probably the least representative of the record as a whole. Yes, it's fun in the moment, but probably not one you'll be caught singing to yourself later. My Love finds Cher channeling Donna Summer in a surreal 70's inspired anthem, and she goes just a little bit country for Favorite Scars, a song that wouldn't be entirely out of place on the Heart of Stone album. Every Cher album is entitled to one true power ballad, and I Hope You Find It takes up that role here. Sirens is a little more low-key, but I love the imagery invoked by the lyrics. If I had to choose, I'd say Dressed to Kill is actually my favorite song on the disc. Despite what others will tell you, she actually reins in the vocoder and techno effects quite a bit on this record compared to prior releases, but they're back in full force on this song that for some reason I can only describe as \\"very European.\\" Truth be told there really aren't any songs here that I would go as far as to call bad, though a couple such as Red and Take it Like a Man didn't really stand out or leave much of an impression on me one way or the other. As far as the exclusive bonus tracks from the red bullseye retailer, the only one I'm super impressed with is the poignant Will You Wait For Me, mostly because I find the lyrics personally relatable.<br /><br />Anyhow, these are just my early thoughts, but I think it's safe to say any existing Cher fan should be satisfied overall and have some fun with this, though I can't honestly say I'm able to picture any of these tracks exploding up the mainstream charts or becoming timeless classics in the vein of Turn Back Time or Believe. Otherwise, she's at the top of her form and can still belt it out like no other. If anything her voice has only gotten deeper since the last time we heard from her, if you can imagine that, lol. Ultimately, those who are touting it as \\"worth the wait\\" or \\"her best CD yet\\" may be a little overzealous and are probably just excited to have something new at all, but it's a solid pop record nevertheless and worth getting a hold of if you have the chance.to Kill is actually my favorite song on the disc. 1594484805|5.0|I am a Licensed Counseling Psychologist that has made his living teaching research-related courses for the last thirty years at several different universities.  I was aware of most of the psychological studies Daniel Pink (a Washington D. C. Attorney) cited as evidence.  I found Daniel Pink's book DRIVE: The Surprising Truth About What Motivates Us, to be a thoughtful, helpful, and encouraging read. Several psychologists would object that the word \\"drive\\" refers to (and should only refer to) physiological needs that \\"push\\" us, as opposed to \\"aspirations\\" or \\"goals\\" that \\"pull\\" us.  But, we can experience any and/or all of them as strong motivational forces within us, so I'm not going to quibble too much about the definitions.<br /><br />Pink says that most corporations are still generally using their versions of the \\"carrot and the stick approach\\" - promising bonuses and otherwise rewarding workers when they get what they want (which can often feel like a \\"bribe\\" to the worker), and using threats, demotions, and firings when they don't get what they want.  These methods will not and cannot release the best things intelligent and creative human beings are capable of, writes Pink.<br /><br />Humans, writes Pink, are mostly motivated by A.M.P.  - Autonomy (enough independence to feel free to do their best work), Mastery (the drive to complete patterns, and achieve the best possible outcomes), and Purpose (to gain a deep sense of personal fulfillment and meaning from consistently doing great work).  Pink argues that corporations/work environments that recognize and build upon these higher human needs and motivations will reap the greater rewards.  His Encarta vs. Wikipedia illustration is powerful and persuasive.<br /><br />I recommended this book to the Chair of our Business Program.  He now has made DRIVE required reading in his Management courses.<br /><br />A close friend of mine is  a \\"turn around specialist\\" for a major restaurant chain.  He tells me that most ofthe hourly staff are really not ready for a full-blown A.M.P. approach, and still need a lot of direction, and sometimes the \\"carrot and stick approach\\" to keep them moving in the right direction.  But, he acknowledges, that the chain's managers would probably respond much better to an A.M.P. approach, versus the \\"carrot and stick approach\\" they usually experience.<br /><br />Even if Pink's book is at times overly repetitious, and at other times a bit too simplistic and over-generalized, I still give it 5 Stars for boldly and creatively reminding us of some of the best (and strongest!) motivations within us, and what we and others can actually achieve, if only given the opportunity.<br /><br />I believe one of my early mentors, psychiatrist Viktor Frankl, would have approved of and appreciated the message of DRIVE.  You can read more about Viktor Frankl in my Kindle book:  [[ASIN:B009JXHJ7Q From Sigmund Freud to Viktor Frankl: How Psychiatry Became Human]].  From any source, it's always good to be reminded that we are not rats or mules, and we and others should not routinely be treated as such.

B008JEJVXG|3.0|Nocturne combines cool 80s new wave stylistic touchstones (some have mentioned Psychedelic Furs; I'd add Here Comes Everybody-era The Wake and a bit of Low Life-era New Order) with a melodic indie/twee-pop sensibility (the Field Mice). You've got electric guitars and bass filtered through generous helpings of chorus and delay and occasionally synthetic-sounding drums co-existing harmoniously with lush, jangle-pop guitars. This may sound a bit anachronistic, but if you're a fan of the era, the references (and the results) are nevertheless satisfying. Even the cover art is an amusing 80s throwback, with a possible reference to the inside of Section 25's Always Now and the same font that's on the back cover and inner sleeve of Duran Duran's Rio. The songs themselves are generally artfully arranged, richly detailed, melodically engaging, and sometimes even wildly catchy.    The only serious drawback lies with the singer, Jack Tatum (who, for all intents and purposes, *is* Wild Nothing), as to these ears he has the light and wispy voice of a barely pubescent 12-year old boy. The sound of his voice ranges from nondescript to mildly annoying. I realize I may be the only person who is bothered by his singing, but let me put it this way: the singers in many of the bands that appear to have influenced this album had highly distinctive, powerful voices that instilled the music with soaring passion, and this kind of music is just crying out for someone like Paul Simpson or Ian McCulloch or even Andy McCluskey to take command of it and really push it into the stratosphere. Even Tatum's lyrics fail to make much of an impression. I really do like the music on Nocturne, but with a better singer this could easily be a 4 star album.

B00DJ80VLS|5.0|A game changer, that is the consistent description that ran through my head as I listened to this album over and over. Not since Scenes of a Memory has any album, let a lone a Dream Theater album, seemed like such a game changer. A bold statement was made by the band when they decided to make this a self-titled album. It is a strong statement that we have yet to see these gentlemen max out their creative spark, but have just begun. And the album lives up to that. Not since When Dream and Day Unite has John Myung been this upfront as a presence in the mix. The \\"silent one\\" is that no more and it is glorious to hear. With the previous album, A Dramatic Turn of Events, I felt James Labrie was finally being considered in the lyric and music creation in that he was given songs that played to his vocal strengths. That continues with this album and in my opinion, his voice sounds better than it ever has. If this album marks a strong presence return for Myung and Labrie Mangini is finally introduced properly here with a thunderous explosion. The last album, he basically filled the drum parts as they were written. This time around, he was a full on part of the process and it shows. Jordan and John P. can't be left out, their ability to write choruses that are beautiful and catchy shine the brightest here. Petrucci's lyrics are on par with his best lyrical album, IMO, Images & Words. Myung and Labrie shinning brightly once again, Mangini fully integrated, Jordan and Petrucci raises the bar even higher, makes this album a game changer and their best overall album, IMO, since Scenes from a Memory.<br /><br />Below I broke down each song to speak to the strengths of each and weaknesses (only one song do I have any real complaint about and it is a minor one):<br /><br />False Awakening Suite - DT decided instead of using previously published music as an intro to their live performances, why not just write something of their own; thus the birth of False Awakening Suite. Equal parts dark and light, this is a perfect introduction to the album.<br /><br />The Enemy Inside - Their lead single and video from the album. This bravado riff heavy track has become one of my favorite tunes of theirs with its attacking style. It is one of the more simple riffs of theirs, yet when all members are fully engaged, it is classic DT. James sounding like James from Images and Words, great all around production. \\"Enemy\\" has an excellent chorus which sets the stage for many more excellent choruses' to come.<br /><br />The Looking Glass - One of two songs that channels DT's Rush influence. They have always toed the line of Rush-esque song writing, but here, they blatantly pay homage to the boys from Canada with an opening riff which is full-on Rush influenced. The result is a great new sound from the band. The only negative I can give for this track is the sudden end of the song. After the final lyric, it just seems to end abruptly.<br /><br />Enigma Machine - The final of two instrumentals on the album. Here, we get an even darker offering than False Awakening Suite. This is one of several songs where Myung is flexing a stronger presence than he has in a very long time in the mix.<br /><br />The Bigger Picture - Continuing the ballad trend from DTOE, the first ballad on the album and an excellent one at that. The last two albums DT has flexed their ability to write softer songs, something we knew from the past they could do, but rarely done this consistently good. Again, a nice chorus highlights an overall strong song. James once again in fine voice here.<br /><br />Behind The Veil - Probably the best chorus of any DT song to date. This song has all of the guys in absolute top form. This is no doubt, my favorite song on the album and probably my favorite song of theirs from the last 15 years. This song reminds me of their work from the first two albums.<br /><br />Surrender To Reason - The final homage to Rush on this album and IMO, the best one. Once again, a strong song powered by a strong chorus. Myung once again, very up front here. There is absolutely no wasted space here. The solo is very much Rush influenced and I can't remember Myung and Petrucci ever having this strong of a solo on a song. Probably the best final 90 seconds of a DT song ever.<br /><br />Along For The Ride - The final ballad on the album. Once again, strong chorus. There is no cheese here. I have always felt musically DT could write ballads with the best of them, but the lyrics were a little \\"processed\\". No longer, lyrics are standing strong with the music consistently now.<br /><br />Illumination Theory - The return of the 20 minute opus. I have to say, this is one of their tightest, best flowing opus' since I&W \\"Metropolis pt1\\". Absolutely beautiful, mid-song orchestra break and once again, a chorus which flows through the whole song and culminates in a grand finale. I love the silent break at the almost 19 minute mark, that sets up the grand finale

In fact, he presents many interesting examples in this book that may well be considered as a celebration of the power of negative thinking.<br /> Among them:* [Attending a motivational seminar featuring Dr. Robert Schuler] *Here it is, then,\\" Dr. Schuller declares, stiffly pacing the stage, which is decorated with two enormous banners reading \\"MOTIVATE\\" and \\"SUCCEED!\\", seventeen American flags, and a large number of potted plants. \\"Here's the thing that will change your life forever.\\" Then he barks a single syllable-\\"Cut!\\"-and leaves a dramatic pause before completing his sentence:  \\" . . . the word impossible out of your life! Cut it off! Cut it out forever!\\" . . .It is only months later, back at my home in New York, reading the headlines over morning coffee, that I learn the news that the largest church in the United States constructed entirely from glass has filed for bankruptcy, a word Dr. Schuller had apparently neglected to eliminate from his vocabulary.I also liked how he cited the work of Albert Ellis:* What Ellis had grasped about his unstated beliefs concerning conversation with women-an insight he would later extend to the beliefs that lie behind all instances of worry or anxiety-is that they were absolutist. To put it another way, it wasn't just that he wanted to be less shy, and that he wanted to be able to talk to women. Rather, he had been operating under the absolutist conviction that he needed their approval. Later, he would coin a name for this habit of mind: \\"musturbation.\\" We elevate those things we want, those things we would prefer to have, into things we believe we must have; we feel we must perform well in certain circumstances, or that other people must treat us well. Because we think these things must occur, it follows that it would be an absolute catastrophe if they did not. No wonder we get anxious: we've decided that if we failed to meet our goal it wouldn't merely be bad, but completely bad-absolutely terrible.And Burkeman shared this neat way of behaving at funerals:* Francisco parked the van, and we walked into the cemetery. It took a moment for my eyes to adjust to what I was seeing. Many of the gravestones were only rough concrete slabs, or stubby pieces of wood, but almost none were unattached. Next to each, sitting in folding chairs, or cross-legged on the ground, were groups of two, three, or four people, sometimes more, holding murmured conversations and drinking tequila from paper cups. In one corner, a mariachi band in full costume strolled from grave to grave, serenading every headstone in turn. I stopped a woman who was carrying armfuls of rugs and chairs towards a nearby headstone, and asked what she was doing. \\"Oh, it's my mother,\\" she said brightly, gesturing at the grave. \\"We come every year.\\"I don't think that THE ANTIDOTE has made me a negative thinker. However, it has made me open to the possibility that there are many different ways to look at happiness.r that other people must treat us well. At just 11 years old, her powers are stronger than ever, and her parents and Danny fear for her safety.<br /><br />Rattlesnake, Rosie the Hat, Snakebite Andi, Crow Daddy, Walnut, Mr. Business Man, Mr. Dealer Man and more, are a motley crew, part of a group called: The True Knot, the Steam Heads.  The group travels cross country in RV clusters, and to the average person, their attire of cheap sun glasses, fishing hats, stretch pants and attention getting bumper stickers makes them appear like many other retirees traveling in RVs.  Believing that they are regular folks would be dangerous, because they really are not interested in seeing the sites in their RV. They have a special agenda, and a special interest in talented young \\"shining\\" ones like Abra.<br /><br />Although Doctor Sleep is a sequel to The Shining, it can be read as a stand-alone novel.  I was happy that there were references to the Overlook Hotel, and also to other characters from The Shining. Most were referred to briefly like: Jack and Wendy (Danny's parents), Richard (Dick) Hallorann, who saved the lives of the Torrance family when the Overlook was destroyed by fire. Also mentioned was Mrs Massey, Room 217, Horace Dorman, as well as Tony, Danny's imaginary friend. It was good to have their names reappear, even briefly in Doctor Sleep.<br /><br />For me, this one is \\"classic Stephen King\\". When I read phrases like \\"eyes dripping and bloated\\",  I smiled. This is the Stephen King, I love the best.  READ IT!n talented young \\"shining\\" ones like Abra.<br /><br />Although Doctor Sleep is a sequel to The Shining, it can be read as a stand-alone novel.
